xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12"><span class="c-article-section__title-number">3.4 </span>Scanning Electron Microscope (SEM)</h3><p>The SEM micrographs see (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig5">5</a>) of the different samples showed that they had structures with considerable particle agglomeration and having size range of 100 to 300&nbsp;nm. Agglomeration reported in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig5">5</a> is common nature of metal oxide after calcination [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Farizan, A.F.; Yusoff, H.M.; Badar, N.; Bhat, I.U.H.; Anwar, S.J.; Wai, C.P.; Asari, A.; Kasim, M.F.; Elong, K.: Green synthesis of magnesium oxide nanoparticles using Mariposa christia vespertilionis leaves extract and its antimicrobial study toward S aureus and E coli. Arab. J. Sci. Eng. (2022). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s13369-022-07282-7
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1387">41</a>]. Particles calcined at 900&nbsp;°C showed the highest level of agglomeration, and this trend continued with higher calcination temperatures. In Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig7">7</a>, the XRD examination revealed a rise in crystallinity and grain size, which is consistent with this observation. The large surface area (active site) of nanoparticles with small crystallite size [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Dodoo-Arhin, D.; Asiedu, T.; Agyei-Tuffour, B.; Nyankson, E.; Obada, D.; Mwabora, J.M.: Photocatalytic degradation of Rhodamine dyes using zinc oxide nanoparticles. Mater. Today: Proceed. 38, 809–815 (2021). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.matpr.2020.04.597
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1393">62</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig5" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="687"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>SEM images of ZnO calcined at <b>a</b> 700&nbsp;ºC, <b>b</b> 800&nbsp;ºC, and <b>c</b> 900&nbsp;ºC; at 10,000X magnification</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 5" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13"><span class="c-article-section__title-number">3.5 </span>Transmission Electron Microscope (TEM)</h3><p>Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig6">6</a> shows TEM images of ZnO-NPs calcined at 700&nbsp;°C, 800&nbsp;°C, and 900&nbsp;°C The TEM observations support the SEM findings about the form and size of the crystallites. The sample that was calcined at 700&nbsp;°C and 800&nbsp;°C had highly agglomerated structures with irregular spherical crystal forms. However, the samples obtained using a longer thermal calcination procedure (900&nbsp;°C) appear to affect the morphology of ZnO-NPs into more irregular polyhedral forms, generating larger particles in larger planar blocks.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6&nbsp;"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6&nbsp;</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig6" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="483"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>TEM image for synthesised ZnO-NPs calcined at 700&nbsp;°C with magnification of <b>a</b> 40&nbsp;k, <b>b</b> 100&nbsp;k, and <b>c</b> 800&nbsp;k; TEM image for synthesised ZnO-NPs calcined at 800&nbsp;°C with magnification of <b>d</b> 40&nbsp;k, <b>e</b> 100&nbsp;k, and <b>f</b> 800&nbsp;k; TEM image for synthesised ZnO-NPs calcined at 900&nbsp;°C with magnification of <b>g</b> 40&nbsp;k, <b>h</b> 100&nbsp;k, and <b>i</b> 800&nbsp;k; SAED patterns are also shown in the insets of the respective HRTEM images of each ZnO-NPs sample</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 6" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The TEM pictures showed that when the calcination temperatures increase, the crystallites will become larger. A sample calcined at 700&nbsp;°C is found to be smaller than a calcined at 900&nbsp;°C, due to more thermal energy available at higher temperatures and crystals can form and growth. This explains why the 900&nbsp;°C sample had bigger crystals than the 700&nbsp;°C sample did. The average size of a crystallite is about 38&nbsp;nm, 42.5&nbsp;nm, and 65.3&nbsp;nm for samples that were heated to 700&nbsp;°C, 800&nbsp;°C, and 900&nbsp;°C, respectively. The sample that was calcined at 900&nbsp;°C had bigger crystals than the samples that were calcined at 700&nbsp;°C and 800&nbsp;°C. This is because the annealing temperature, which is 900&nbsp;°C, is higher. Higher temperatures always make it easier for crystals to grow faster because there is more energy available for phase formation and crystal growth [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Singh, J.; Kumar, S.; Alok, A.; Upadhyay, S.K.; Rawat, M.; Tsang, D.C.W.; Bolan, N.; Kim, K.-H.: The potential of green synthesized zinc oxide nanoparticles as nutrient source for plant growth. J. Clean. Prod. 214, 1061–1070 (2019). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jclepro.2019.01.018
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1485">33</a>].</p><p>Selected area electron diffraction was also used to look at the structure and crystal orientations of the ZnO-NPs (SAED). Insets of the HRTEM images of each ZnO-NPs sample show the SAED patterns (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig6">6</a>c, f, and i). The ZnO-NPs are made up of many different crystals, which can be seen in the pattern of the SAED. Samples that were calcined at 700&nbsp;°C, 800&nbsp;°C, and 900&nbsp;°C have interplanar distances of 0.2517&nbsp;nm, 0.2532&nbsp;nm, and 0.2556&nbsp;nm, respectively. These distances are the same as the (002) plane spacing. The XRD results show that ZnO-NPs have a hexagonal wurtzite structure. This (002) crystal plane proves that.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14"><span class="c-article-section__title-number">3.6 </span>X-ray Diffraction (XRD)</h3><p>All the diffraction peaks are in the range 20° until 80°, indicating a hexagonal wurtzite structure for the ZnO-NPs confirmed by PDF reference 8,901,397 (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig7">7</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="de Almeida, W.L.; Ferreira, N.S.; Rodembusch, F.S.; de Sousa, V.C.: Study of structural and optical properties of ZnO nanoparticles synthesized by an eco-friendly tapioca-assisted route. Mater. Chem. Phys. 15(258), 123926 (2021). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.matchemphys.2020.123926
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1505">63</a>]. The intensity of the diffraction peaks increases as the calcination temperature rises, indicating that the ZnO phase is becoming stronger [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="de Almeida, W.L.; Ferreira, N.S.; Rodembusch, F.S.; de Sousa, V.C.: Study of structural and optical properties of ZnO nanoparticles synthesized by an eco-friendly tapioca-assisted route. Mater. Chem. Phys. 15(258), 123926 (2021). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.matchemphys.2020.123926
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1508">63</a>]. The (100), (002), and (101) planes correspond to the three sharp peaks. Using the Debye–Scherrer, Eq.&nbsp;(<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Equ1">1</a>), the average size of Zn-ONPs was determined based on the most intense peak (101). Which is D, the crystalline size in nm, k is the Scherrer constant equal to 0.9, <i>λ</i> is x-ray wavelength (0.15406&nbsp;nm), while β is the full width at half maximum (FWHM) of the peak in radians, and <i>θ</i> is the peak location in radians. From the calculation, the data show the commercial ZnO typically 63&nbsp;nm size crystallites, while ZnO-NPs calcined at 700, 800 and 900&nbsp;°C have 17&nbsp;nm, 29&nbsp;nm and 55&nbsp;nm size crystallites, respectively.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Fig. 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig7" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure 7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="577"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>XRD diffractograms of ZnO at <b>a</b> commercial, <b>b</b> 900 ºC and <b>c</b> 800 ºC, and <b>d</b> 700&nbsp;°C</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 7" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" class="mathjax-tex">$$D=\frac{k\lambda }{\beta \mathrm{cos}\theta }$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (1)
                </div></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15"><span class="c-article-section__title-number">3.7 </span>Accelerated Surface Area and Porosimeter (ASAP) Analysis</h3><p>Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig8">8</a> shows the adsorption–desorption isotherms plot of ZnO-NPs at different calcined temperatures (700&nbsp;°C, 800&nbsp;°C, and 900&nbsp;°C). Therefore, based from the isotherm plots, all the ZnO-NPs samples are in type IV adsorption isotherms by the IUPAC classification, which are associated with mesoporous structures with pore sizes ranging from about 2&nbsp;nm to 50&nbsp;nm [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Ismail, M.A.; Taha, K.K.; Modwi, A.; Khezami, L.: Zno nanoparticles: surface and x-ray profile analysis. J. Ovonic Res. 14, 381–393 (2018)" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1605">64</a>]. The hysteresis type of ZnO-NPs is H3 which described solid shapes with very wide distribution of pore size as shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig8">8</a> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Saravanan, M.; Gopinath, V.; Chaurasia, M.K.; Syed, A.; Ameen, F.; Purushothaman, N.: Green synthesis of anisotropic zinc oxide nanoparticles with antibacterial and cytofriendly properties. Microb. Pathog. 115, 57–63 (2018). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.micpath.2017.12.039
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1611">23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Pourrahimi, A.M.; Liu, D.; Strom, V.; Hedenqvist, M.S.; Olsson, R.T.; Gedde, U.W.: Heat treatment of ZnO nanoparticles: new methods to achieve high-purity nanoparticles for high-voltage applications. J. Mater. Chem. 3, 17190–17200 (2015). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1039/c5ta03120f
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1614">65</a>]. The relative pressure of the desorption branch step area leading to a lower closure point is almost completely influenced by the adsorption. Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Tab3">3</a> shows the SBET of ZnO-NPs samples annealed at different temperatures measured by N2 gas adsorption. As a results, ZnO-NPs showed highest surface area when calcined at 800&nbsp;°C and lowest surface area calcined at 900&nbsp;°C. At lower calcination temperatures (700–800&nbsp;°C), surface area and pore volume increased gradually owing to the degradation of the precursor. Lower calcination temperatures, as stated in the thermal study, may have led to the evolution of distinct gases from the surface and bulk of the precursors, resulting in more porous structures and an increase in surface area. However, at higher calcination temperatures 900&nbsp;°C, the surface area and pore volume of NPs decrease owing to agglomeration, crystallite development, and inter-crystallite sintering; this is consistent with the literature [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Shohel, M.; Miran, M.S.; Susan, M.A.B.H.; Mollah, M.Y.A.: Calcination temperature-dependent morphology of photocatalytic ZnO nanoparticles prepared by an electrochemical–thermal method. Res. Chem. Intermed. 42, 5281–5297 (2015). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s11164-015-2358-x
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1621">66</a>]. ZnO-NPs' large surface area allows for enhanced electrode interface–analyte interaction and easier electron transport during the oxidation process [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Heydari, M.; Ghoreishi, S.M.; Khoobi, A.: Novel electrochemical procedure for sensitive determination of Sudan II based on nanostructured modified electrode and multivariate optimization. Measurement 142, 105–112 (2019). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.measurement.2019.04.058
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1624">67</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Fig. 8"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 8</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" <b>b</b> 800&nbsp;°C and <b>c</b> 900&nbsp;oC</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16"><span class="c-article-section__title-number">3.8 </span>UV–Visible (UV–Vis) Spectrometer</h3><p>The wavelength range of 200–800&nbsp;nm at room temperature using UV–Vis absorption spectrum from Shimadzu cooperation is recorded all peak ZnO-NPs at 377&nbsp;nm. UV–Vis peak for all ZnO-NPs dispersed in distilled water is shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig9">9</a> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Yusoff, H.M.; Idris, N.H.; Hipul, N.F.; Yusoff, M.; Izham, Z.M.; Bhat, I.U.H.: Green synthesis of zinc oxide nanoparticles using black tea extract and its potential as anode material in sodium-ion batteries. Malays. J. Chem. 22(2), 43–51 (2020)" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1795">26</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Zhu, W.; Hu, C.; Ren, Y.; Lu, Y.; Song, Y.; Ji, Y.; Han, C.; He, J.: Green synthesis of zinc oxide nanoparticles using Cinnamomum camphora (L) Presl leaf extracts and its antifungal activity. Environ. Chem. Eng. (2021). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jece.2021.106659
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1798">68</a>]. As stated by a previous study, the range of 360–380&nbsp;nm is the maximum optical absorption band [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Yusoff, H.M.; Idris, N.H.; Hipul, N.F.; Yusoff, M.; Izham, Z.M.; Bhat, I.U.H.: Green synthesis of zinc oxide nanoparticles using black tea extract and its potential as anode material in sodium-ion batteries. Malays. J. Chem. 22(2), 43–51 (2020)" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1801">26</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Zhu, W.; Hu, C.; Ren, Y.; Lu, Y.; Song, Y.; Ji, Y.; Han, C.; He, J.: Green synthesis of zinc oxide nanoparticles using Cinnamomum camphora (L) Presl leaf extracts and its antifungal activity. Environ. Chem. Eng. (2021). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jece.2021.106659
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1804">68</a>]. The observed all absorption curve are 377&nbsp;nm (3.27&nbsp;eV), which is close to the band gap of the ZnO 1&nbsp;s-1&nbsp;s electron transition (3.37&nbsp;eV) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Suresh, D.; Nethravathi, P.C.; Rajanaika, H.; Nagabhushana, H.; Sharma, S.C.: Green synthesis of multifunctional zinc oxide (ZnO) nanoparticles using Cassia fistula plant extract and their photodegradative, antioxidant and antibacterial activities. Mater. Sci. Semiconduct. Process. 1(31), 446–454 (2015). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.mssp.2014.12.023
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1808">69</a>]. Above 3.3&nbsp;eV absorption in ZnO assembled to the band-to-band transition. As particle size decreases, so does the distance between electronic levels and the band gap. This is due to the electron hole pairs being significantly closer together. Furthermore, the coulombic interaction between them cannot be missed, resulting in a larger total kinetic energy [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Nath, M.R.; Ahmed, A.N.; Gafur, M.A.; Miah, M.Y.; Bhattacharjee, S.: ZnO nanoparticles preparation from spent zinc–carbon dry cell batteries: studies on structural, morphological and optical properties. J. Asian Ceramic Soc. 6, 262–270 (2018). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/21870764.2018.1507610
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1811">70</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-9" data-title="Fig. 9"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig9" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 9</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/9" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig9_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig9" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig9_HTML.png" alt="figure 9" loading="lazy" width="685" height="531"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-9-desc"><p>UV–Visible spectra of ZnO calcined at 700, 800 and 900 ºC</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/9" data-track-dest="link:Figure9 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 9" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17"><span class="c-article-section__title-number">3.9 </span>Cyclic Voltammograms (CV)</h3><p>Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was used to examine the electrochemical performance of a ZnO-NPs. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig10">10</a> shows CV curves measured at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s-1. The ZnO-NPs calcined at 700&nbsp;°C was chosen due to the highest initial charge capacity and good cycling stability after 100 cycles. CV is used to evaluate the electrochemical properties of ZnO structures as anode materials for Sodium-ion batteries. The CV curves for five cycles are shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig10">10</a>. Two cathodic peaks, one at 0.35&nbsp;V (major peak) and the other at 0.98&nbsp;V (minor peak), are seen in the first cycle. Addition of Na<sup>+</sup> into the carbon is responsible for the 0.35&nbsp;V peak, whereas breakdown of the electrolyte causes the 0.98&nbsp;V and the production of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). Subsequent cycles showed no sign of the 0.35&nbsp;V peak, indicating that the 0.35&nbsp;V peak was caused by dominated conversion/alloying processes. The multistep de-sodiation of the NaZn alloy to create ZnO is responsible for the appearance of numerous peaks at distinct potentials, 0.09, 1.47, and 2.86&nbsp;V, in the anodic region during the first cycle. Equation&nbsp;(<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Equ1">1</a>)-(<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Equ1">2</a>) represent the ZnO conversion/alloying and sodiation insertion reactions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Duraisamy, E.; Prabunathan, P.; Mani, G.; Alshgari, R.A.; Elumalai, P.: [Zn(Salen)] metal complex-derived ZnO-implanted carbon slabs as anode material for lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries. Mater. Chem. Front. 5, 3886–3896 (2021). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1039/d0qm01031f
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1854">71</a>]. The pattern obtained in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig10">10</a> represents conversion and sodiation reaction [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Nath, M.R.; Ahmed, A.N.; Gafur, M.A.; Miah, M.Y.; Bhattacharjee, S.: ZnO nanoparticles preparation from spent zinc–carbon dry cell batteries: studies on structural, morphological and optical properties. J. Asian Ceramic Soc. 6, 262–270 (2018). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1080/21870764.2018.1507610
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e1860">70</a>].</p><div id="Equ2" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\text{ZnO}}\, + \,{\text{2 Na}}\, + \,\, + \,{\text{2e}} - \, \leftrightarrow \,{\text{Na2O}}\, + \,{\text{Zn}}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (2)
                </div></div><div id="Equ3" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\text{Zn}}\, + \,{\text{Na}}\, + \,\, + \,{\text{e}} - \, \leftrightarrow \,{\text{NaZn}}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (3)
                </div></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-10" data-title="Fig. 10"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig10" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 10</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/10" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig10_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig10" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig10_HTML.png" alt="figure 10" loading="lazy" width="685" height="550"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-10-desc"><p>The first five ZnO-NPs electrode cyclic voltammograms in the 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V potential window at scan rate 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/10" data-track-dest="link:Figure10 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 10" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec18"><span class="c-article-section__title-number">3.9.1 </span>Electrochemical Performance of ZnO-NPs</h4><p>The cycling stability of the produced ZnO-NPs was further examined by monitoring their cycle performance up to 100 cycles.</p><p>ZnO-NPs calcined at 700, 800, and 900&nbsp;°C have different cycling performances, as shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig11">11</a>a. A voltage range of 0.01–3.00&nbsp;V was used to perform 0.1 C rate cycling on the test cells [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Durai, L.; Moorthy, B.; Issac Thomas, C.; Kyung Kim, D.; Kamala Bharathi, K.: Electrochemical properties of BiFeO3 nanoparticles: Anode material for sodium-ion battery application. Mater. Sci. Semicond 68, 165–171 (2017). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.mssp.2017.06.003
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e2019">72</a>]. The highest&nbsp;irreversible discharge capacity of 591 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was observed in ZnO-NPs calcined at 700&nbsp;°C, followed by 440 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in NPs calcined at 900&nbsp;°C and 355 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in NPs calcined at 800&nbsp;°C at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The reversible discharge of ZnO-NPs calcined at 700&nbsp;°C is 128 mAh g-<sup>1</sup> through second cycle and drops to 94 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles with 73% capacity retention. The reversible discharge capacity of ZnO-NPs calcined at 900&nbsp;°C decreased from 120 mAh g-<sup>1</sup> for second cycle to 80 mAh g-<sup>1</sup> for 100th cycle, with 66% retention rate. This may be Na-ions have shorter diffusion lengths in ZnO due to the material's small particle sizes, which also increases the active sites for ion insertation/de-insertation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Durai, L.; Moorthy, B.; Issac Thomas, C.; Kyung Kim, D.; Kamala Bharathi, K.: Electrochemical properties of BiFeO3 nanoparticles: Anode material for sodium-ion battery application. Mater. Sci. Semicond 68, 165–171 (2017). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.mssp.2017.06.003
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e2039">72</a>]. In comparison, the reversible discharge capacity of ZnO-NPs calcined at 800&nbsp;°C was 96 mAh g-<sup>1</sup> at the second cycle and dropped to 59 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles with 62% retention capacity. Moreover, furthermore, the initial galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the ZnO-NPs at current density of 100 mAhg-1 are displayed in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig11">11</a>(b), (c), and (d) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Durai, L.; Moorthy, B.; Issac Thomas, C.; Kyung Kim, D.; Kamala Bharathi, K.: Electrochemical properties of BiFeO3 nanoparticles: Anode material for sodium-ion battery application. Mater. Sci. Semicond 68, 165–171 (2017). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.mssp.2017.06.003
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e2050">72</a>]. As can be seen, the initial discharge curves are distinct from subsequent discharge curves for all electrodes, indicating the formation of an SEI layer during the initial sodiation. As a result, all electrodes experience irreversible capacity loss during the initial cycle, resulting in a low initial Coulombic efficiency [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Mahamad Yusoff, N.F.; Idris, N.H.; Md Din, M.F.; Majid, S.R.; Harun, N.A.; Rahman, M.M.: Electrochemical sodiation/desodiation into Mn3O4 nanoparticles. ACS Omega 5(45), 29158–29167 (2020). 
                  https://doi.org/10.1021/acsomega.0c03888
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e2053">49</a>]. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig11">11</a>b shows that the plateau voltages of the charge–discharge profiles match well with the peak voltage in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig10">10</a> CV.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-11" data-title="Fig. 11"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig11" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 11</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/11" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig11_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig11" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig11_HTML.png" alt="figure 11" loading="lazy" width="685" height="542"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-11-desc"><p><b>a</b> Cyclability of ZnO-NPs calcined at 700&nbsp;ºC, 800&nbsp;ºC, and 900&nbsp;ºC over 100 cycles; discharge profile <b>b</b> 700&nbsp;ºC, <b>c</b> 800&nbsp;ºC, and <b>d</b> 900&nbsp;ºC</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/11" data-track-dest="link:Figure11 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 11" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The discharge performance of the half cells at various current densities that range from 0.2 C to 1.0 C is shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#Fig12">12</a>. ZnO-NPs for 700&nbsp;°C exhibits a discharge capacity of around 349 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> at a low rate of 0.2 C. However, the discharge capacity gradually drops and after 66 cycles discharge capacity of about 58 mAhg<sup>−1</sup>. Nevertheless, this sample ZnO-NPs practically recovered their original capacities when the rate was again dropped to 0.2 C after being exposed to large current loads (1.0 C), which illustrates the remarkable stability of ZnO-NPs [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Yepuri, V.; Kuchimanchi, D.; Senthil, C.; Sasidharan, M.: Synthesis of zno hollow nanospheres and investigation of their electrochemical reactivity for lithium-ion batteries. In: Proceedings of National Conference on Emerging Interdisciplinary Trends of Chemical Technology (2014). 
                  https://doi.org/10.13140/2.1.4646.4968
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d87991586e2102">73</a>]. Evidently, ZnO-NPs at a calcination temperature of 700&nbsp;°C show the highest initial discharge and had good cycling stability after 66 cycles. At ZnO-NPs calcined at 800&nbsp;°C show the lowest initial discharge, 169 mAhg<sup>−1</sup>. After 66 cycles, the discharge capacity is 44 mAhg<sup>−1</sup>. While ZnO-NPs 900&nbsp;°C show moderately initial discharge which is 325 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> and after 66 cycles is 54 mAhg<sup>−1</sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-12" data-title="Fig. 12"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig12" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 12</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/12" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig12_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig12" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13369-023-08300-y/MediaObjects/13369_2023_8300_Fig12_HTML.png" alt="figure 12" loading="lazy" width="685" height="564"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-12-desc"><p>Rate capability at different current density of ZnO-NPs at 700&nbsp;ºC, 800&nbsp;ºC, and 900&nbsp;ºC</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s13369-023-08300-y/figures/12" data-track-dest="link:Figure12 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 12" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec19-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec19"><span class="c-article-section__title-number">4 </span>Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec19-content"><p>In conclusion, ZnO-NPs were effectively synthesised at calcination temperatures of 700, 800, and 900&nbsp;°C by a green synthesis method that is straightforward, environmentally friendly, and non-hazardous. At 580&nbsp;°C, TGA analysis showed no substantial weight loss for these samples. FTIR spectroscopy analysis revealed Zn–O stretching at 594&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. The XRD diffractograms revealed that the produced ZnO-NPs had a highly crystalline, hexagonal wurtzite structure following calcination. SEM images having size range of 100 to 300&nbsp;nm. While for TEM ZnO-NPs material formed at 700&nbsp;°C had the smallest particle size and the highest surface area. ZnO-NPs calcined at 700&nbsp;°C&nbsp;displayed higher specific capacities, excellent cyclability, and rate capability than others calcination temperatures.</p></div></div></section>
                                </div>
